<p>Many Walmart technology projects are coded in the open and available through the Walmart Labs <a href="/wiki/GitHub" title="GitHub">GitHub</a> repository<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/Open_Source" class="mw-redirect" title="Open Source">Open Source</a> software under the <a href="/wiki/Open_Source_Initiative" title="Open Source Initiative">OSI approved</a> <a href="/wiki/Apache_License" title="Apache License">Apache V2.0 license</a>. At the time of writing (November 2016), 141 public Github projects are listed.</p>
<p>During a migration of the walmart.com retail platform to <a href="/wiki/React_(JavaScript_library)" title="React (JavaScript library)">Facebook React</a> and <a href="/wiki/Node.js" title="Node.js">Node.js</a>, the Electrode<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> project was created to power the e-commerce platform which serves 80 million visitors per month and 15 million items.</p>
<p>Electrode provides various developer enhancements and tools for the developer including Node.js configuration and feature management.</p>
<p>Alex Grigoryan<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> of Walmart Labs released a statement<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> on Medium.com 3 October 2016 explaining the details of the applications and the scale that they operate at Walmart.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_data_analytics">Big data analytics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Big data analytics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As the largest retailer in the U.S., Walmart collects and analyzes a large amount of consumer data. The <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">big data</a> sets are <a href="/wiki/Data_mining" title="Data mining">mined</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Predictive_analytics" title="Predictive analytics">predictive analytics</a>, which allow the company to optimize operations by predicting customer's habits. Walmart's <a href="/wiki/Datacenter" class="mw-redirect" title="Datacenter">datacenter</a> is unofficially referred to as <a href="/wiki/Area_71" title="Area 71">Area 71</a>.</p>
<p>In April 2011, Walmart acquired <a href="/wiki/Kosmix" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosmix">Kosmix</a> to develop software for analyzing real-time data streams.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> In August 2012, Walmart announced its Polaris search engine.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>The amount of data gathered by Walmart has raised privacy concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Charity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sam Walton believed that the company's contribution to society was the fact that it operated efficiently, thereby lowering the <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_living" title="Cost of living">cost of living</a> for customers, and, therefore, in that sense was a "powerful force for good", despite his refusal to contribute cash to philanthropic causes.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Having begun to feel that his wealth attracted people who wanted nothing more than a "handout", he explained that while he believed his family had been fortunate and wished to use his wealth to aid worthy causes like education, they could not be expected to "solve every personal problem that comes to [their] attention". He explained later in his autobiography, "We feel very strongly that Wal-Mart really is <i>not</i>, and <i>should not</i> be, in the charity business," stating "any debit has to be passed along to somebody‍—‌either shareholders or our customers."<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> Since Sam Walton's death in 1992, however, Walmart and the Walmart Foundation dramatically increased charitable giving. For example, in 2005, Walmart donated <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$20<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million</span> in cash and merchandise for <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> relief. Today, Walmart's charitable donations approach <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span> each year.<sup id="cite_ref-Charity_Evaluated_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charity_Evaluated-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Economic impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Kenneth Stone, Professor of Economics at <a href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University" title="Iowa State University">Iowa State University</a>, in a paper published in <i>Farm Foundation</i> in 1997, found that some small towns can lose almost half of their retail trade within ten years of a Walmart store opening.<sup id="cite_ref-Rural_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rural-44">[44]</a></sup> He compared the changes to previous competitors small town shops have faced in the past‍—‌from the development of the railroads and the Sears Roebuck catalog to shopping malls. He concludes that small towns are more affected by "discount mass merchandiser stores" than larger towns and that shop owners who adapt to the ever-changing retail market can "co-exist and even thrive in this type of environment."<sup id="cite_ref-Rural_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rural-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>One study found Walmart's entry into a new market has a profound impact on its competition. When a Walmart opens in a new market, median sales drop 40 percent at similar high-volume stores, 17 percent at supermarkets and 6 percent at <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy" title="Pharmacy">drugstores</a>, according to a June 2009 study by researchers at several universities and led by the Tuck School of Business at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Loyola_University_Chicago" title="Loyola University Chicago">Loyola University Chicago</a> study suggested that the impact a Walmart store has on a local business is correlated to its distance from that store. The leader of that study admits that this factor is stronger in smaller towns and doesn't apply to more urban areas saying "It'd be so tough to nail down what's up with Wal-Mart".<sup id="cite_ref-wpost_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wpost-215">[215]</a></sup> These findings are underscored by another study conducted in 2009 by the National Bureau of <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">Economics</a> that showed "large, negative effects" for competing businesses within five to ten <a href="/wiki/Miles" class="mw-redirect" title="Miles">miles</a> of the new opening big box retailer. This same study also found that the local retailers experience virtually no benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> This particularly contrasts with studies that find that local firms re-invest nearly 63% more of <a href="/wiki/Profit_(accounting)" title="Profit (accounting)">profits</a> in other local businesses compared to chain retailers, as found by the <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> Center of Economic Policy in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>Walmart has been subject to criticism from various groups and individuals, including <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor unions</a>, community groups, <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">grassroots</a> organizations, religious organizations, environmental groups, and the company's own customers and employees. They have protested against the company's policies and business practices, including charges of racial and gender discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-mkabel_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mkabel-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Other areas of criticism include the company's foreign product sourcing, treatment of suppliers, employee compensation and working conditions, environmental practices, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_welfare" title="Corporate welfare">the use of public subsidies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labor_spies#Wal-Mart_surveillance_of_employees" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor spies">the company's security policies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> Walmart denies doing anything wrong and maintains that low prices are the result of efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>A 2001 <a href="/wiki/McKinsey_%26_Company" title="McKinsey &amp; Company">McKinsey</a> Global Institute study of U.S. labor productivity growth between 1995 and 2000 concluded that "Wal-Mart directly and indirectly caused the bulk of the productivity acceleration" in the retail sector.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Solow" title="Robert Solow">Robert Solow</a>, a Nobel laureate in economics and an adviser to the study, stated that "[b]y far the most important factor in that [growth] is Wal-Mart."<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Economic_Policy_Institute" title="Economic Policy Institute">Economic Policy Institute</a> estimates that between 2001 and 2006, Wal-Mart's trade deficit with China alone eliminated nearly 200,000 U.S. jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Another study at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">University of Missouri</a> found that a new store increases net retail employment in the county by 100 jobs in the short term, half of which disappear over five years as other retail establishments close.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2004 paper by two professors at <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a> found that U.S. counties with Walmart stores suffered increased poverty compared with counties without Wal-Marts.<sup id="cite_ref-PSUstudy_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PSUstudy-230">[230]</a></sup> They hypothesized that this could be due to the displacement of workers from higher-paid jobs in the retailers customers no longer choose to patronize, Wal-Mart providing less local charity than the replaced businesses, or a shrinking pool of local leadership and reduced <a href="/wiki/Social_capital" title="Social capital">social capital</a> due to a reduced number of local independent businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-PSUstudy_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PSUstudy-230">[230]</a></sup> Dr <a href="/wiki/Raj_Patel" title="Raj Patel">Raj Patel</a>, author of "<a href="/wiki/Stuffed_and_Starved" title="Stuffed and Starved">Stuffed and Starved</a>: Markets, Power and the Hidden Battle for the World Food System", said in a lecture at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Melbourne" title="University of Melbourne">University of Melbourne</a> on September 18, 2007, that a study in Nebraska looked at two different Wal-Marts, the first of which had just arrived and "was in the process of driving everyone else out of business but, to do that, they cut their prices to the bone, very, very low prices". In the other Wal-Mart, "they had successfully destroyed the local economy, there was a sort of economic crater with Wal-Mart in the middle; and, in that community, the prices were 17<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span> percent higher".<sup id="cite_ref-RNBigIdeas_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RNBigIdeas-231">[231]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2005 story in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> reported that "Wal-Mart's discounting on food alone boosts the welfare of American shoppers by at least <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$50<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span> per year."<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> A study in 2005 at the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT) measured the effect on <a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">consumer welfare</a> and found that the poorest segment of the population benefits the most from the existence of discount retailers.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></li>
<li>A June 2006 article published by the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises_Institute" title="Ludwig von Mises Institute">Ludwig von Mises Institute</a> suggested that Wal-Mart has a positive impact on small business.<sup id="cite_ref-ultimate_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ultimate-234">[234]</a></sup> It argued that while Wal-Mart's low prices caused some existing businesses to close, the chain also created new opportunities for other small business, and so "the process of <a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">creative destruction</a> unleashed by Wal-Mart has no statistically significant impact on the overall size of the small business sector in the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-sobel_dean_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sobel_dean-235">[235]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2006, American newspaper columnist <a href="/wiki/George_Will" title="George Will">George Will</a> named Wal-Mart "the most prodigious job-creator in the history of the private sector in this galaxy" and that "[b]y lowering consumer prices, Wal-Mart costs about 50 retail jobs among competitors <i>for every 100 jobs Wal-Mart creates</i>". In terms of economic effects, Will states that "Wal-Mart and its effects save shoppers more than <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$200<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span> a year, dwarfing such government programs as <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Nutrition_Assistance_Program" title="Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program">food stamps</a> (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$28.6<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span>) and the <a href="/wiki/Earned_income_tax_credit" title="Earned income tax credit">earned income tax credit</a> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$34.6<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span>)".<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2014 story in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported that the Wal-Mart Foundation was boosting its efforts to work with U.S. manufacturers. In February 2014, the Walmart Foundation pledged <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span> to support domestic manufacturers and announced plans to buy <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$250<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion</span> worth of American-made products in the next decade.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_relations">Labor relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Labor relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="604.70857142857" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons">
<source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (60 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="59718" /></audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Workers speak during <a href="/wiki/Occupy_Wall_Street" title="Occupy Wall Street">Occupy Wall Street</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With over 2.2 million employees worldwide, Walmart has faced a torrent of lawsuits and issues with regards to its workforce. These issues involve <a href="/wiki/Wage" title="Wage">low wages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occupational_safety_and_health" title="Occupational safety and health">poor working conditions</a>, inadequate <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a>, as well as issues involving the company's strong <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">anti-union</a> policies. In November 2013, the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Board" title="National Labor Relations Board">National Labor Relations Board</a> (NLRB) announced that it had found that in 13<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>U.S. states Wal-Mart had pressured employees not to engage in strikes on Black Friday, and had illegally disciplined workers who had engaged in strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> Critics point to Walmart's high <a href="/wiki/Turnover_(employment)" title="Turnover (employment)">turnover</a> rate as evidence of an unhappy workforce, although other factors may be involved. Approximately 70<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>percent of its employees leave within the first year.<sup id="cite_ref-Store_Wars_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Store_Wars-239">[239]</a></sup> Despite this turnover rate, the company is still able to affect unemployment rates. This was found in a study by Oklahoma State University which states, "Walmart is found to have substantially lowered the relative unemployment rates of blacks in those counties where it is present, but to have had only a limited impact on relative incomes after the influences of other socio-economic variables were taken into account."<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>Walmart is the largest private employer in the United States, employing almost five times as many people as <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, the second largest employer.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> Walmart employs more <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> than any other private employer in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT4115_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT4115-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 23, 2015, Walmart announced that it is bumping its minimum wage to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10</span><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>an<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>hour by February 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_and_sexual_orientation">Gender and sexual orientation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Gender and sexual orientation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2007, a <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">gender discrimination</a> lawsuit, <i><a href="/wiki/Dukes_v._Wal-Mart_Stores,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Dukes v. Wal-Mart Stores, Inc.">Dukes v. Wal-Mart Stores, Inc.</a></i>, was filed against Walmart, alleging that female employees were discriminated against in matters regarding pay and promotions. A <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a> suit was sought, which would have been the nation's largest in history, covering 1.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million past and current employees.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenhouse_07_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhouse_07-244">[244]</a></sup> On June 20, 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">United States Supreme Court</a> ruled in Wal-Mart's favor, stating that the plaintiffs did not have enough in common to constitute a class.<sup id="cite_ref-SCOTUS_Walmart_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCOTUS_Walmart-245">[245]</a></sup> The court ruled unanimously that because of the variability of the plaintiffs' circumstances, the class action could not proceed as presented, and furthermore, in a 5–4 decision that it could not proceed as any kind of class action suit.<sup id="cite_ref-Lennard_Natasha_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lennard_Natasha-246">[246]</a></sup> Several plaintiffs, including the lead plaintiff, Betty Dukes, expressed their intent to file individual discrimination lawsuits separately.<sup id="cite_ref-Clifford_Stephanie_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford_Stephanie-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a consultant hired by plaintiffs in a sex discrimination lawsuit, in 2001, Wal-Mart's EEOC filings showed that female employees made up 65 percent of Wal-Mart's hourly paid workforce, but only 33<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>percent of its management.<sup id="cite_ref-Conlin_01_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conlin_01-248">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zellner_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zellner-249">[249]</a></sup> Just 35<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>percent of its store managers were women, compared to 57 percent at similar retailers.<sup id="cite_ref-zellner_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zellner-249">[249]</a></sup> Wal-Mart says comparisons with other retailers are unfair, because it classifies employees differently; if department managers were included in the totals, women would make up 60<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>percent of the managerial ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-zellner_249-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zellner-249">[249]</a></sup> Others have criticized the lawsuit as without basis in the law and as an abuse of the class action mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> In 2007, Wal-Mart was named by the National Association for Female Executives as one of the top<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>35 companies for Executive Women.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wal-Mart's rating on the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Campaign" title="Human Rights Campaign">Human Rights Campaign</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Corporate_Equality_Index" title="Corporate Equality Index">Corporate Equality Index</a>, a measure of how companies treat <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> employees and customers, has increased greatly during the past decade.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> The company was praised for expanding its anti-discrimination policy protecting gay and lesbian employees,<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> as well as for a new definition of "family" that included same-sex partners.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EqualityIndex16_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EqualityIndex16-258">[258]</a></sup> However, they have been criticized by the HRC in other areas, such as not renewing its membership in the National Gay and Lesbian Chamber of Commerce.<sup id="cite_ref-washblade_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washblade-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2006, Wal-Mart announced that "diversity efforts include new groups of minority, female and gay employees that meet at Wal-Mart headquarters in Bentonville to advise the company on marketing and internal promotion. There are seven Business Resource Groups: women, African-Americans, Hispanics, Asians, Native Americans, Gays and Lesbians, and a disabled group."<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare">Animal welfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Animal welfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In April 2016, Walmart announced that it plans to eliminate eggs from <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cages</a> from its supply chain by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> The decision was particularly important because of Walmart's large <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> and influence on the rest of the industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FortuneEgg_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FortuneEgg-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BloombergEgg_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloombergEgg-263">[263]</a></sup> The move was praised by major <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> groups<sup id="cite_ref-Modern_Farmer_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Farmer-264">[264]</a></sup> and heralded as an "end of an era" by <a href="/wiki/Humane_Society_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Humane Society of the United States">HSUS</a> president <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Pacelle" title="Wayne Pacelle">Wayne Pacelle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> but a poultry trade group representative expressed skepticism about the decision's impact.<sup id="cite_ref-Modern_Farmer_264-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Farmer-264">[264]</a></sup> Walmart's cage-free eggs will not come from <a href="/wiki/Free_range" title="Free range">free range</a> producers, but rather <a href="/wiki/Factory_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Factory farming">industrial-scale farms</a> where the birds will be allotted between 1 and 1.5 square feet each, a stressful arrangement which can cause <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_in_poultry" title="Cannibalism in poultry">cannibalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FortuneEgg_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FortuneEgg-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Modern_Farmer_264-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Farmer-264">[264]</a></sup> Unlike battery cages, the systems Walmart's suppliers will use allow the hens to move around, but relative to battery cages they have higher hen mortality rates and present distinct environmental and worker health problems.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_Gelles_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_Gelles-266">[266]</a></sup></p>

But Walmart has been so successful with such material that it's now become a business threat to Christian booksellers.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWalmart&amp;rft.atitle=9+religious+companies+%28besides+Chick-fil-A%29&amp;rft.aufirst=Dan&amp;rft.aulast=Gilgoff&amp;rft.date=2012-07-24&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Freligion.blogs.cnn.com%2F2012%2F07%2F24%2F7-religious-companies-besides-chick-fil-a%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
